            function hasConnection() {
                return navigator.connection || navigator.mozConnection || navigator.webkitConnection || navigator.msConnection;
            }

            function createLink(src) {
                var preloadLink = document.createElement("link");
                preloadLink.rel = "preload";
                preloadLink.href = src;
                preloadLink.as = "font";
                preloadLink.type = "font/woff2";
                preloadLink.crossOrigin = "";
                document.head.insertBefore(preloadLink, document.head.firstChild);
            }

            var connectionDetail = {
                saveDataEnabled: false,
                slowConnection: false
            };

            var connection = hasConnection();
            if (connection) {
                connectionDetail.saveDataEnabled = connection.saveData;
                if (/\slow-2g|2g/.test(connection.effectiveType)) {
                    connectionDetail.slowConnection = true;
                }
            }

            if (!(connectionDetail.saveDataEnabled || connectionDetail.slowConnection)) {
                createLink("/static/fonts/HardingText-Regular-Web-cecd90984f.woff2");
            } else {
                document.documentElement.classList.add('save-data');
            }

<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    (function () {
        var urlParams = new URLSearchParams(window.location.search);
        if (urlParams.get('gptAdsTest') !== null) {
            document.addEventListener('sncc:initialise', function(event) {
                var gptScriptSource = 'https://pagead2.googlesyndication.com/tag/js/gpt.js';
                if (event.detail.C03 === true) {
                    gptScriptSource = 'https://securepubads.g.doubleclick.net/tag/js/gpt.js';
                }
                var gptScript = document.createElement('script');
                gptScript.type = 'text/javascript';
                gptScript.src = gptScriptSource;
                gptScript.async = false;
                gptScript.onload = function () {
                    var gtmScript = document.createElement('script');

<script data-test="gtm-head">
    window.initGTM = function() {
        if (window.config.mustardcut) {
            (function (w, d, s, l, i) {
                w[l] = w[l] || [];
                w[l].push({'gtm.start': new Date().getTime(), event: 'gtm.js'});
                var f = d.getElementsByTagName(s)[0],
                        j = d.createElement(s),
                        dl = l != 'dataLayer' ? '&l=' + l : '';
                j.async = true;
                j.src = 'https://sgtm.nature.com/gtm.js?id=' + i + dl;
                f.parentNode.insertBefore(j, f);
            })(window, document, 'script', 'dataLayer', 'GTM-MRVXSHQ');
        }
    }
</script>

    <script>
    (function(w,d,t) {
        function cc() {
            var h = w.location.hostname;
            if (h === 'preview-www.nature.com') return;
            var e = d.createElement(t),
                s = d.getElementsByTagName(t)[0];
            if (h === 'nature.com' || h.endsWith('.nature.com')) {
                e.src = 'https://cmp.nature.com/production_live/en/consent-bundle-8-100.js';
                e.setAttribute('onload', "initGTM(window,document,'script','dataLayer','GTM-MRVXSHQ')");
            } else {
                e.src = '/static/js/cookie-consent-es5-bundle-8d962b73c2.js';
                e.setAttribute('data-consent', h);
            }
            s.insertAdjacentElement('afterend', e);
        }
        cc();
    })(window,document,'script');
</script>


<script id="js-position0">
    (function(w, d) {
        w.idpVerifyPrefix = 'https://verify.nature.com';
        w.ra21Host = 'https://wayf.springernature.com';
        var moduleSupport = (function() {
            return 'noModule' in d.createElement('script');
        })();

        if (w.config.mustardcut === true) {
            w.loader = {
                index: 0,
                registered: [],
                scripts: [
                    
                        {src: '/static/js/global-article-es6-bundle-e979e7e7bc.js', test: 'global-article-js', module: true},
                        {src: '/static/js/global-article-es5-bundle-597536b0e3.js', test: 'global-article-js', nomodule: true},
                        {src: '/static/js/shared-es6-bundle-0c7392804a.js', test: 'shared-js', module: true},
                        {src: '/static/js/shared-es5-bundle-f97043df39.js', test: 'shared-js', nomodule: true},
                        {src: '/static/js/header-150-es6-bundle-5bb959eaa1.js', test: 'header-150-js', module: true},
                        {src: '/static/js/header-150-es5-bundle-994fde5b1d.js', test: 'header-150-js', nomodule: true}
                    
                ].filter(function (s) {
                    if (s.src === null) return false;
                    if (moduleSupport && s.nomodule) return false;
                    return !(!moduleSupport && s.module);
                }),

                register: function (value) {
                    this.registered.push(value);
                },

                ready: function () {
                    if (this.registered.length === this.scripts.length) {
                        this.registered.forEach(function (fn) {
                            if (typeof fn === 'function') {
                                setTimeout(fn, 0); 
                            }
                        });
                        this.ready = function () {};
                    }
                },

                insert: function (s) {
                    var t = d.getElementById('js-position' + this.index);
                    if (t && t.insertAdjacentElement) {
                        t.insertAdjacentElement('afterend', s);
                    } else {
                        d.head.appendChild(s);
                    }
                    ++this.index;
                },

                createScript: function (script, beforeLoad) {
                    var s = d.createElement('script');
                    s.id = 'js-position' + (this.index + 1);
                    s.setAttribute('data-test', script.test);
                    if (beforeLoad) {
                        s.defer = 'defer';
                        s.onload = function () {
                            if (script.noinit) {
                                loader.register(true);
                            }
                            if (d.readyState === 'interactive' || d.readyState === 'complete') {
                                loader.ready();
                            }
                        };
                    } else {
                        s.async = 'async';
                    }
                    s.src = script.src;
                    return s;
                },

                init: function () {
                    this.scripts.forEach(function (s) {
                        loader.insert(loader.createScript(s, true));
                    });

                    d.addEventListener('DOMContentLoaded', function () {
                        loader.ready();
                        var conditionalScripts;
                        
                            conditionalScripts = [
                                {match: 'div[data-pan-container]', src: '/static/js/pan-zoom-es6-bundle-464a2af269.js', test: 'pan-zoom-js',  module: true },
                                {match: 'div[data-pan-container]', src: '/static/js/pan-zoom-es5-bundle-98fb9b653b.js', test: 'pan-zoom-js',  nomodule: true },
                                {match: 'math,span.mathjax-tex', src: '/static/js/math-es6-bundle-55688a0084.js', test: 'math-js', module: true},
                                {match: 'math,span.mathjax-tex', src: '/static/js/math-es5-bundle-6a270012ec.js', test: 'math-js', nomodule: true}
                            ];
                        

                        if (conditionalScripts) {
                            conditionalScripts.filter(function (script) {
                                return !!document.querySelector(script.match) && !((moduleSupport && script.nomodule) || (!moduleSupport && script.module));
                            }).forEach(function (script) {
                                loader.insert(loader.createScript(script));
                            });
                        }
                    }, false);
                }
            };
            loader.init();
        }
    })(window, document);
</script>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="To systematically understand age-induced molecular changes, we performed spatial transcriptomics of young, middle-aged, and old mouse brains and identified seven transcriptionally distinct regions. All regions exhibited age-associated upregulation of inflammatory mRNAs and downregulation of mRNAs related to synaptic function. Notably, aging white matter fiber tracts showed the most prominent changes with pronounced effects in females. The inflammatory signatures indicated major ongoing events: microglia activation, astrogliosis, complement activation, and myeloid cell infiltration. Immunofluorescence and quantitative MRI analyses confirmed physical interaction of activated microglia with fiber tracts and concomitant reduction of myelin in old mice. In silico analyses identified potential transcription factors influencing these changes. Our study provides a resourceful dataset of spatially resolved transcriptomic features in the naturally aging murine brain encompassing three age groups and both sexes. The results link previous disjointed findings and provide a comprehensive overview of brain aging identifying fiber tracts as a focal point of inflammation. This study profiles the spatial transcriptomic landscape of mouse brain coronal sections across three age groups and both sexes. Here, authors show a strong female-biased pro-inflammatory gene signature in white matter fiber tracts."/>

    <meta name="description" content="To systematically understand age-induced molecular changes, we performed spatial transcriptomics of young, middle-aged, and old mouse brains and identified seven transcriptionally distinct regions. All regions exhibited age-associated upregulation of inflammatory mRNAs and downregulation of mRNAs related to synaptic function. Notably, aging white matter fiber tracts showed the most prominent changes with pronounced effects in females. The inflammatory signatures indicated major ongoing events: microglia activation, astrogliosis, complement activation, and myeloid cell infiltration. Immunofluorescence and quantitative MRI analyses confirmed physical interaction of activated microglia with fiber tracts and concomitant reduction of myelin in old mice. In silico analyses identified potential transcription factors influencing these changes. Our study provides a resourceful dataset of spatially resolved transcriptomic features in the naturally aging murine brain encompassing three age groups and both sexes. The results link previous disjointed findings and provide a comprehensive overview of brain aging identifying fiber tracts as a focal point of inflammation. This study profiles the spatial transcriptomic landscape of mouse brain coronal sections across three age groups and both sexes. Here, authors show a strong female-biased pro-inflammatory gene signature in white matter fiber tracts."/>

Irfanoglu</a><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff3">3</a></sup>, </li><li class="c-article-author-list__item c-article-author-list__item--hide-small-screen"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" data-track-index="8_21" data-track-context="researcher popup with no profile" href="#auth-James_R_-Occean-Aff1-Aff9" data-author-popup="auth-James_R_-Occean-Aff1-Aff9" data-author-search="Occean, James R.">James R. Occean</a><span class="u-js-hide">
            <a class="js-orcid" href="https://orcid.org/0000-0002-6823-6381"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0002-6823-6381</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff1">1</a></sup><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#nAff9">nAff9</a></sup>, </li><li class="c-article-author-list__item c-article-author-list__item--hide-small-screen"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" data-track-index="9_21" data-track-context="researcher popup with no profile" href="#auth-Sadia-Afrin-Aff1" data-author-popup="auth-Sadia-Afrin-Aff1" data-author-search="Afrin, Sadia">Sadia Afrin</a><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff1">1</a></sup>, </li><li class="c-article-author-list__item c-article-author-list__item--hide-small-screen"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" data-track-index="10_21" data-track-context="researcher popup with no profile" href="#auth-Nishat-Sarker-Aff1" data-author-popup="auth-Nishat-Sarker-Aff1" data-author-search="Sarker, Nishat">Nishat Sarker</a><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff1">1</a></sup>, </li><li class="c-article-author-list__item c-article-author-list__item--hide-small-screen"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" data-track-index="11_21" data-track-context="researcher popup with no profile" href="#auth-Ross_A_-McDevitt-Aff4" data-author-popup="auth-Ross_A_-McDevitt-Aff4" data-author-search="McDevitt, Ross A.">Ross A. McDevitt</a><span class="u-js-hide">
            <a class="js-orcid" href="https://orcid.org/0000-0003-3722-9047"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0003-3722-9047</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff4">4</a></sup>, </li><li class="c-article-author-list__item c-article-author-list__item--hide-small-screen"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" data-track-index="12_21" data-track-context="researcher popup with no profile" href="#auth-Elin-Lehrmann-Aff5" data-author-popup="auth-Elin-Lehrmann-Aff5" data-author-search="Lehrmann, Elin">Elin Lehrmann</a><span class="u-js-hide">
            <a class="js-orcid" href="https://orcid.org/0000-0002-9869-9475"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0002-9869-9475</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff5">5</a></sup>, </li><li class="c-article-author-list__item c-article-author-list__item--hide-small-screen"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" data-track-index="13_21" data-track-context="researcher popup with no profile" href="#auth-Shahroze-Abbas-Aff6" data-author-popup="auth-Shahroze-Abbas-Aff6" data-author-search="Abbas, Shahroze">Shahroze Abbas</a><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff6">6</a></sup>, </li><li class="c-article-author-list__item c-article-author-list__item--hide-small-screen"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" data-track-index="14_21" data-track-context="researcher popup with no profile" href="#auth-Nirad-Banskota-Aff5" data-author-popup="auth-Nirad-Banskota-Aff5" data-author-search="Banskota, Nirad">Nirad Banskota</a><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff5">5</a></sup>, </li><li class="c-article-author-list__item c-article-author-list__item--hide-small-screen"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" data-track-index="15_21" data-track-context="researcher popup with no profile" href="#auth-Jinshui-Fan-Aff5" data-author-popup="auth-Jinshui-Fan-Aff5" data-author-search="Fan, Jinshui">Jinshui Fan</a><span class="u-js-hide">
            <a class="js-orcid" href="https://orcid.org/0009-0001-9300-1920"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0009-0001-9300-1920</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff5">5</a></sup>, </li><li class="c-article-author-list__item c-article-author-list__item--hide-small-screen"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" data-track-index="16_21" data-track-context="researcher popup with no profile" href="#auth-Supriyo-De-Aff5" data-author-popup="auth-Supriyo-De-Aff5" data-author-search="De, Supriyo">Supriyo De</a><span class="u-js-hide">
            <a class="js-orcid" href="https://orcid.org/0000-0002-2075-7655"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0002-2075-7655</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff5">5</a></sup>, </li><li class="c-article-author-list__item c-article-author-list__item--hide-small-screen"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" data-track-index="17_21" data-track-context="researcher popup with no profile" href="#auth-Peter-Rapp-Aff7" data-author-popup="auth-Peter-Rapp-Aff7" data-author-search="Rapp, Peter">Peter Rapp</a><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff7">7</a></sup>, </li><li class="c-article-author-list__item c-article-author-list__item--hide-small-screen"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" data-track-index="18_21" data-track-context="researcher popup with no profile" href="#auth-Arya-Biragyn-Aff2" data-author-popup="auth-Arya-Biragyn-Aff2" data-author-search="Biragyn, Arya">Arya Biragyn</a><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff2">2</a></sup>, </li><li class="c-article-author-list__item c-article-author-list__item--hide-small-screen"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" data-track-index="19_21" data-track-context="researcher popup with no profile" href="#auth-Dan-Benjamini-Aff7" data-author-popup="auth-Dan-Benjamini-Aff7" data-author-search="Benjamini, Dan">Dan Benjamini</a><span class="u-js-hide">
            <a class="js-orcid" href="https://orcid.org/0000-0002-9558-7056"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0002-9558-7056</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff7">7</a></sup>, </li><li class="c-article-author-list__item c-article-author-list__item--hide-small-screen"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" data-track-index="20_21" data-track-context="researcher popup with no profile" href="#auth-Manolis-Maragkakis-Aff1" data-author-popup="auth-Manolis-Maragkakis-Aff1" data-author-search="Maragkakis, Manolis">Manolis Maragkakis</a><span class="u-js-hide">
            <a class="js-orcid" href="https://orcid.org/0000-0002-3158-1763"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0002-3158-1763</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff1">1</a></sup> &amp; </li><li class="c-article-author-list__show-more" aria-label="Show all 21 authors for this article" title="Show all 21 authors for this article"></li><li class="c-article-author-list__item"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" data-track-index="21_21" data-track-context="researcher popup with no profile" href="#auth-Payel-Sen-Aff1" data-author-popup="auth-Payel-Sen-Aff1" data-author-search="Sen, Payel" data-corresp-id="c1">Payel Sen<svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-mail-medium"></use></svg></a><span class="u-js-hide">
            <a class="js-orcid" href="https://orcid.org/0000-0003-2809-0901"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0003-2809-0901</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff1">1</a></sup></li></ul><button aria-expanded="false" class="c-article-author-list__button"><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-chevron-down-medium"></use></svg><span>Show authors</span></button>

        <div class="c-article-body">
            <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>To systematically understand age-induced molecular changes, we performed spatial transcriptomics of young, middle-aged, and old mouse brains and identified seven transcriptionally distinct regions. All regions exhibited age-associated upregulation of inflammatory mRNAs and downregulation of mRNAs related to synaptic function. Notably, aging white matter fiber tracts showed the most prominent changes with pronounced effects in females. The inflammatory signatures indicated major ongoing events: microglia activation, astrogliosis, complement activation, and myeloid cell infiltration. Immunofluorescence and quantitative MRI analyses confirmed physical interaction of activated microglia with fiber tracts and concomitant reduction of myelin in old mice. In silico analyses identified potential transcription factors influencing these changes. Our study provides a resourceful dataset of spatially resolved transcriptomic features in the naturally aging murine brain encompassing three age groups and both sexes. The results link previous disjointed findings and provide a comprehensive overview of brain aging identifying fiber tracts as a focal point of inflammation.</p></div></div></section>

                <div class="main-content">
                    
                        <section data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>It is estimated that 50 million people worldwide have neurodegenerative diseases, and by 2050, this number will triple<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Collaborators, G. B. D. D. F. Estimation of the global prevalence of dementia in 2019 and forecasted prevalence in 2050: an analysis for the Global Burden of Disease Study 2019. Lancet Public Health 7, e105e125 (2022)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e761">1</a></sup>. Age is the strongest risk factor for many neurodegenerative conditions, including late-onset sporadic Alzheimers disease and related dementias (ADRD), Parkinsons disease (PD), amyotrophic lateral sclerosis (ALS), etc<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Hou, Y. et al. Ageing as a risk factor for neurodegenerative disease. Nat. Rev. Neurol. 15, 565581 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e765">2</a></sup>. Brain aging is accompanied by gross morphological changes and cognitive deficits that likely contribute to disease onset<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Bethlehem, R. A. I. et al. Brain charts for the human lifespan. Nature 604, 525533 (2022)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e769">3</a></sup>. Mapping the progressive cellular and molecular alterations that drive age-related cognitive decline is thus critical for designing ameliorative therapies. Importantly, there is an emphasis on more diagnostic (after disease) over pre-diagnostic (aging but no disease) experimentations that prevent our understanding of disease incidence mechanisms.</p><p>Aging and age-related diseases show spatial bias in most complex tissues, including the brain, due to differences in blood flow, metabolic demands, and chemoattractant or chemorepellent signals. Recent studies have only begun to reveal some interesting spatial signatures of aging. Cells responding to acute or chronic injury have been shown to organize into cellular neighborhoods and reside in niches that depend on the presence or absence of other cells. For example, in the aging liver, the zone 3 pericentral hepatocytes undergo accelerated aging<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Hall, D. M. et al. Aging reduces adaptive capacity and stress protein expression in the liver after heat stress. J. Appl Physiol. 89, 749759 (2000)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e776">4</a></sup> and have the greatest neoplastic potential<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Kurosaki, S. et al. Cell fate analysis of zone 3 hepatocytes in liver injury and tumorigenesis. JHEP Rep. 3, 100315 (2021)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e780">5</a></sup>. In the injured kidney, immune-active altered cellular niches form in the proximal tubules and thick ascending limbs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Lake, B. B. et al. An atlas of healthy and injured cell states and niches in the human kidney. Nature 619, 585594 (2023)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e784">6</a></sup>. In aged skeletal muscle, cell types are mislocalized and show signs of both early injury and late regeneration<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Wang, Y. X. et al. A single cell spatial temporal atlas of skeletal muscle reveals cellular neighborhoods that orchestrate regeneration and become disrupted in aging. Preprint at bioRxiv &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1101/2022.06.10.494732&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2022)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e788">7</a></sup>. In AD brains, transcriptional changes occur around amyloid plaques<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Chen, W. T. et al. Spatial transcriptomics and in situ sequencing to study Alzheimers disease. Cell 182, 976991.e19 (2020)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e792">8</a></sup>.</p><p>A common spatial signature across tissues during injury or aging is the activation of a localized immune response (sterile inflammation) concurrent with productive or maladaptive repair. The brain is an otherwise immune-privileged organ due to the presence of the bloodbrain barrier (BBB), a tightly controlled system of blood vessels that allow only select agents to pass through. Thus, immune activation in the aging or diseased brain is of particular interest and may contribute to the rapid loss of neurons and cognitive impairment. Several brain-resident cell types have been implicated in this pro-inflammatory response, including microglia, astrocytes, and oligodendrocytes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Lucin, K. M. &amp; Wyss-Coray, T. Immune activation in brain aging and neurodegeneration: too much or too little? Neuron 64, 110122 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e799">9</a></sup>. Alternatively, or simultaneously, age-related BBB deterioration can cause peripheral immune cell infiltration and an aggravated inflammatory response<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Knox, E. G., Aburto, M. R., Clarke, G., Cryan, J. F. &amp; ODriscoll, C. M. The blood-brain barrier in aging and neurodegeneration. Mol. Psychiatry 27, 26592673 (2022)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e803">10</a></sup>.</p><p>To comprehensively understand brain aging at the cellular and molecular level, we undertook a deep spatiotemporal profiling of aging coronal brain sections from both male and female mice. Our data captured previously known and unknown transcriptomic changes as a function of age and sex and identified the white matter fiber tract (hereon fiber tract) as a key anatomical region vulnerable to age-related inflammation. We present our work as a resource to the community for interrogation of age-related disease mechanisms in the brain.</p></div></div></section><section data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Profiling the aging murine brain using spatial transcriptomics</h3><p>Spatial transcriptomics (ST) can reveal mRNA differences, paracrine signaling, and cellular networks not captured in averaged spatially naive analyses of homogenized biopsies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Rao, A., Barkley, D., Franca, G. S. &amp; Yanai, I. Exploring tissue architecture using spatial transcriptomics. Nature 596, 211220 (2021)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e822">11</a></sup>. This prospect of multi-dimensional in situ output motivated us to use ST in aging mouse brains across 3 age groups (young (Y, ~11 weeks), middle-aged (M, ~57.5 weeks), and old (O, ~126 weeks)) and both sexes of C57BL/6JN mice (Supp. Data<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM3">1</a>). Each age group contained four biological replicates (males (M) <i>n</i>=2 and females (F) <i>n</i>=2) and 2 technical replicates (A or B) i.e., independent, succeeding coronal sections from the same OCT block, for a total of 24 samples (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig1">1A</a>, steps 1 and 2).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Spatial transcriptomic profiles partition the brain into discrete anatomical regions."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: Spatial transcriptomic profiles partition the brain into discrete anatomical regions.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-025-58466-2/MediaObjects/41467_2025_58466_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="Fig1" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-025-58466-2/MediaObjects/41467_2025_58466_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure 1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="857"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>A</b> Overview of ST workflow using the 10X Genomics Visium platform. <b>B</b> Representative Visium image of O2FB (top, <i>n</i>=3419 spots) showing spatial transcriptional profiles colored by cluster-based annotation (bottom). <b>C</b> Heatmap of top ten marker mRNAs (count mean values) in young, middle-aged, and old (left) or male and female (right) groups unique to each region. <b>D</b> Allen Brain Atlas P56<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Allen Reference Atlas. Mouse brain [brain atlas]. &#xA;                  atlas.brain-map.org&#xA;                  &#xA;                ." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e865">14</a></sup> coronal section images from <a href="http://mouse.brain-map.org/static/atlas">http://mouse.brain-map.org/static/atlas</a> showing the spatial locations of the seven major regions identified in (<b>C</b>). The relevant links are: Isocortex: <a href="https://atlas.brain-map.org/atlas?atlas=1&amp;plate=100960084#atlas=1&amp;plate=100960084&amp;resolution=20.94&amp;x=5519.9993896484375&amp;y=3904.8334765434265&amp;zoom=-4&amp;structure=315&amp;z=6">https://atlas.brain-map.org/atlas?atlas=1&amp;plate=100960084#atlas=1&amp;plate=100960084&amp;resolution=20.94&amp;x=5519.9993896484375&amp;y=3904.8334765434265&amp;zoom=-4&amp;structure=315&amp;z=6</a>. Hypothalamus: <a href="https://atlas.brain-map.org/atlas?atlas=1&amp;plate=100960084#atlas=1&amp;plate=100960084&amp;resolution=20.94&amp;x=5519.9993896484375&amp;y=3904.8334765434265&amp;zoom=-4&amp;structure=1097&amp;z=6">https://atlas.brain-map.org/atlas?atlas=1&amp;plate=100960084#atlas=1&amp;plate=100960084&amp;resolution=20.94&amp;x=5519.9993896484375&amp;y=3904.8334765434265&amp;zoom=-4&amp;structure=1097&amp;z=6</a>. Thalamus: <a href="https://atlas.brain-map.org/atlas?atlas=1&amp;plate=100960084#atlas=1&amp;plate=100960084&amp;resolution=20.94&amp;x=5519.9993896484375&amp;y=3904.8334765434265&amp;zoom=-4&amp;structure=549&amp;z=6">https://atlas.brain-map.org/atlas?atlas=1&amp;plate=100960084#atlas=1&amp;plate=100960084&amp;resolution=20.94&amp;x=5519.9993896484375&amp;y=3904.8334765434265&amp;zoom=-4&amp;structure=549&amp;z=6</a>. OLF.CTXsp: <a href="https://atlas.brain-map.org/atlas?atlas=1&amp;plate=100960084#atlas=1&amp;plate=100960084&amp;resolution=20.94&amp;x=5519.9993896484375&amp;y=3904.8334765434265&amp;zoom=-4&amp;structure=698&amp;z=6">https://atlas.brain-map.org/atlas?atlas=1&amp;plate=100960084#atlas=1&amp;plate=100960084&amp;resolution=20.94&amp;x=5519.9993896484375&amp;y=3904.8334765434265&amp;zoom=-4&amp;structure=698&amp;z=6</a>. <a href="https://atlas.brain-map.org/atlas?atlas=1&amp;plate=100960084#atlas=1&amp;plate=100960084&amp;resolution=20.94&amp;x=5519.9993896484375&amp;y=3904.8334765434265&amp;zoom=-4&amp;structure=703&amp;z=6">https://atlas.brain-map.org/atlas?atlas=1&amp;plate=100960084#atlas=1&amp;plate=100960084&amp;resolution=20.94&amp;x=5519.9993896484375&amp;y=3904.8334765434265&amp;zoom=-4&amp;structure=703&amp;z=6</a>. Fiber tract: <a href="https://atlas.brain-map.org/atlas?atlas=1&amp;plate=100960084#atlas=1&amp;plate=100960084&amp;resolution=20.94&amp;x=5519.9993896484375&amp;y=3927.9999804496765&amp;zoom=-4&amp;structure=1009&amp;z=6">https://atlas.brain-map.org/atlas?atlas=1&amp;plate=100960084#atlas=1&amp;plate=100960084&amp;resolution=20.94&amp;x=5519.9993896484375&amp;y=3927.9999804496765&amp;zoom=-4&amp;structure=1009&amp;z=6</a>. Hippocampus: <a href="https://atlas.brain-map.org/atlas?atlas=1&amp;plate=100960084#atlas=1&amp;plate=100960084&amp;resolution=20.94&amp;x=5519.9993896484375&amp;y=3904.8334765434265&amp;zoom=-4&amp;structure=1080&amp;z=6">https://atlas.brain-map.org/atlas?atlas=1&amp;plate=100960084#atlas=1&amp;plate=100960084&amp;resolution=20.94&amp;x=5519.9993896484375&amp;y=3904.8334765434265&amp;zoom=-4&amp;structure=1080&amp;z=6</a>. CNU/striatum: <a href="https://atlas.brain-map.org/atlas?atlas=1&amp;plate=100960084#atlas=1&amp;plate=100960084&amp;resolution=20.94&amp;x=5519.9993896484375&amp;y=3904.8334765434265&amp;zoom=-4&amp;structure=623&amp;z=6">https://atlas.brain-map.org/atlas?atlas=1&amp;plate=100960084#atlas=1&amp;plate=100960084&amp;resolution=20.94&amp;x=5519.9993896484375&amp;y=3904.8334765434265&amp;zoom=-4&amp;structure=623&amp;z=6</a>. <b>E</b> Visium images (top) and UMAP (bottom) visualization of seven major regions showing the expression of representative well-known region-specific marker mRNAs in an old female sample (O2FB). Numbers in the color scale reflect the number of UMIs detected for the specified mRNA for each spot. <b>F</b> Same as (<b>E</b>) except profiles generated using a non-cluster-based method, SpatialDE (see Methods). Illustration credit for parts of (<b>A</b>) goes to Endosymbiont GmbH, and BioRender. Sen, P. (2025) <a href="https://BioRender.com/u24a349">https://BioRender.com/u24a349</a>. Source data are provided as a Source Data file.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 1" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-chevron-right-small"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We used the Visium ST platform (10X Genomics) to measure total mRNA in intact tissue sections. The Visium slides contain spatial barcodes that retrospectively link gene activity to its location. Our choice for the Visium platform was motivated by greater tissue coverage, resolution, and throughput compared to existing techniques such as laser microdissection RNA-seq or in situ hybridization<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Moses, L. &amp; Pachter, L. Museum of spatial transcriptomics. Nat. Methods 19, 534546 (2022)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e968">12</a></sup>. Prior to working with real samples, we performed a careful permeabilization optimization on one young sample (Supp. Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM1">1A</a>). Mouse brain sections (16m) were fixed, stained, and permeabilized for different lengths of time (330min) on a tissue optimization slide (see Methods). mRNA released during permeabilization bound to capture probes on the optimization slide with minimum lateral diffusion. cDNA was generated using fluorescently labeled nucleotides to enable visualization by microscopy. After fluorescence imaging, we selected 30min as the optimum permeabilization time as it showed the maximum fluorescence signal and the lowest signal diffusion. The brightfield image confirmed that the lack of or suboptimal fluorescence signal at other time points was due to insufficient permeabilization and not missing tissue. This optimized permeabilization time was applied to all experimental sections to release mRNA on a Visium spatial gene expression slide.</p><p>Each Visium spatial gene expression slide contains 4 capture areas, with each area containing ~5000 barcoded spots, being about 55m in diameter, and covering ~525 cells per spot for a 16m section. We randomized tissue sections and hemispheres from each age group over several gene expression slides (Supp. Data<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM4">2</a>). Tissue sections were first stained with Hematoxylin-Eosin (H&amp;E), followed by cDNA synthesis, amplification, barcoding, and library construction (see Methods). Following this demonstrated protocol, we obtained high-quality spatially resolved RNA-seq data from these 24 samples and sequenced them on the NovaSeq 6000 platform achieving an average coverage of 171 million read pairs per sample, in line with recommendations (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig1">1A</a>, steps 36). Representative bioanalyzer traces post-cDNA amplification and library PCR are shown in Supp Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM1">1B</a>. We observed the expected average fragment size and yield of cDNA (top) and libraries (bottom) for each sample. After next generation sequencing, we processed the data through Space Ranger (10X Genomics) which aligned, extracted barcodes, counted unique molecular identifiers (UMIs), and compiled a feature-barcode matrix for each sample. Space Ranger was further used to assess quality control metrics which revealed an average of 3590 spots under the tissue (~72% coverage of 5000 spots), including an average of 45,995 mean reads per spot and 4179 median genes per barcode (Supp. Data<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM4">2</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Spatial transcriptomic profiles partition the brain into discrete anatomical regions</h3><p>We next imported the feature-barcode matrix from Space Ranger into Partek Flow Genomic Analysis Software (Partek Inc.)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Partek Inc. (2020). Partek Flow (Version 10.0.23.0531 or 11.0.23.1204) [Computer software]. &#xA;                  https://www.partek.com/partek-flow/&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2020)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e998">13</a></sup> for further analysis. The data were first preprocessed by filtering out low-quality spots, removing lowly expressed mRNAs (value 1.0 in at least 99.0% of spots), defining thresholds for mitochondrial (035%) and ribosomal (014%) RNA counts (Supp. Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM1">1C, D</a>), variance stabilizing by log normalization and finally normalizing RNA counts to sequencing depth (counts per million or CPM) (Supp. Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM1">1E</a>). This filtered list contained 10109 mRNAs (Supp. Data<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM5">3</a>). A correlation analysis of counts across these ~10,000 mRNAs showed high concordance (Pearsons <i>r</i>=0.931) between technical and biological replicates and even among different age groups suggesting few global transcriptional changes in the brain with age.</p><p>We next performed principal component analysis (PCA) to reduce the dimensionality of the data followed by graph-based clustering using the Louvain algorithm (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig1">1B top</a>). We annotated the clusters to the captured H&amp;E image of each sample and fine-tuned the fit by adjusting the resolution and manually correcting any misidentified spots (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig1">1B</a> bottom). Remarkably, the clusters neatly classified into seven different brain anatomical regions as seen in the Allen Mouse Brain Atlas<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Allen Reference Atlas. Mouse brain [brain atlas]. &#xA;                  atlas.brain-map.org&#xA;                  &#xA;                ." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e1023">14</a></sup>; striatum/cerebral nuclei (CNU), isocortex, olfactory region with cortical subplate (OLF.CTXsp), fiber tracts, hippocampus, hypothalamus, and thalamus (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig1">1C, D</a>). The top ten marker mRNAs unique to each cluster are shown for young, middle-aged, and old age (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig1">1C</a>, left) and male and female (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig1">1C</a>, right) groups. Some marker mRNAs based on the clustering into seven anatomical regions are annotated to the histological sections and shown in Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig1">1E</a>. We used an orthogonal and non-clustering-based approach called SpatialDE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Svensson, V., Teichmann, S. A. &amp; Stegle, O. SpatialDE: identification of spatially variable genes. Nat. Methods 15, 343346 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e1040">15</a></sup>, to map mRNA profiles across the mouse brain. SpatialDE uses a Bayesian hierarchical model and assumes that the mRNA levels vary smoothly across the tissue, and that nearby cells are more similar in their expression profiles than distant cells. SpatialDE also identified distinct transcriptomic profiles across the seven anatomical regions of the mouse brain (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig1">1F</a>).</p><p>Overall, both approaches confirmed that spatial transcriptomic profiles partition the brain into distinct anatomical regions which then allowed us to compare mRNA profiles in these different regions across age and sex groups. The number of spots covered by each region was proportional to its size (Supp. Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM1">1F</a>), and there were no observable differences between the groups based on age (young, middle, and old) (Supp. Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM1">1G</a>) or sex (male and female) (Supp. Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM1">1H</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Anatomical regions of the brain display both shared and unique sets of age-correlated mRNAs</h3><p>At first pass, we used ANOVA to identify differentially abundant mRNAs across all brain regions (akin to pseudobulk transcriptomics) in the three age groups. We classified an mRNA to be differentially abundant (DAR) in one age group if the false discovery rate from the BenjaminiHochberg method (FDR) &lt;0.05 and fold change (FC) 1.5 (upregulated) or 1.5 (downregulated) (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig2">2A, B</a>, Supp. Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM1">2A, B</a>, and Supp. Data<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM6">4</a>). DARs upregulated with age showed a distinct immune activation signature and included mRNAs related to antigen processing and presentation such as proteasome 20S subunit beta 8 (<i>Psmb8</i> mRNA) and cathepsin H (<i>Ctsh</i> mRNA), microglia activation such as allograft inflammatory factor (<i>Aif1</i> mRNA), galectin-3 (<i>Lgals3</i> mRNA), cluster of differentiation 68 (<i>Cd68</i> mRNA), triggering receptor expressed on myeloid cells 2 (<i>Trem2</i> mRNA), tyrosine kinase-binding protein (<i>Tyrobp</i> mRNA), lysozyme 2 (<i>Lyz2</i> mRNA), glycoprotein non-metastatic melanoma protein B-like protein (<i>Gpnmb</i> mRNA), and secreted phosphoprotein 1 (<i>Spp1</i> mRNA), complement system activation such as complement proteins 1q (<i>C1q</i> mRNA) and 4b (<i>C4b</i> mRNA), reactive astrogliosis such as serine peptidase inhibitor, clade A member 3N (<i>Serpina3n</i> mRNA), S100 calcium binding protein A4 (<i>S100a4</i> mRNA), glial fibrillary acidic protein (<i>Gfap</i> mRNA) and vimentin (<i>Vim</i> mRNA), as well as neuronal excitation and synaptic plasticity such as leucine-rich glioma inactivated 4 (<i>Lgi4</i> mRNA), cluster of differentiation 9 (<i>Cd9</i> mRNA), S100 calcium binding protein A4 (<i>S100a4</i> mRNA) and nuclear protein transcriptional regulator 1 (<i>Nupr1</i> mRNA). By contrast, DARs downregulated with age were related to brain development and adult neurogenesis, such as growth hormone (<i>Gh</i> mRNA), fatty acid binding protein 7 (<i>Fabp7</i> mRNA), neuronal regeneration-related protein (<i>Nrep</i> mRNA), and Kruppel-like factor 10 (<i>Klf10</i> mRNA). The expanded Gene Ontology (GO) terms associated with these age-related DARs are shown in Supp. Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM1">2CE</a> for three-way comparison between young, middle-aged, and old samples. When plotting the pseudobulk DARs across the three age groups, it was evident that a majority were gradually upregulated with age (Supp. Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM1">3</a>, clusters 1, 2, and 4, <i>n</i>=149), with much of the increase occurring in the oldest age group. A smaller group of mRNAs were gradually downregulated with age (Supp. Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM1">3</a>, clusters 910, <i>n</i>=23).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Anatomical regions of the brain display both shared and unique sets of age-correlated mRNAs."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: Anatomical regions of the brain display both shared and unique sets of age-correlated mRNAs.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-025-58466-2/MediaObjects/41467_2025_58466_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="Fig2" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-025-58466-2/MediaObjects/41467_2025_58466_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure 2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="887"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>A</b> Volcano plot showing -log<sub>10</sub>(FDR) and log<sub>2</sub>(FC) values for all 10109 mRNAs with 149 significantly upregulated (purple dots; FDR &lt;0.05, FC 1.5) and 30 significantly downregulated (blue dots; FDR &lt;0.05, FC 1.5) DARs in old vs young. <b>B</b> Strip chart showing log<sub>2</sub>FC value for all 10109 mRNAs across seven regions with significantly upregulated and significantly downregulated (as in (<b>A</b>)) DARs highlighted in each region. <b>C</b> Bar plot showing the number of regions in which a given DAR was detected (top), and number of DARs that were either significantly upregulated or downregulated (as in (<b>A</b>)) with age in each region (bottom). <b>D</b> Heatmap showing log<sub>10</sub>FC values for age-correlated mRNAs across at least two brain regions. <b>E</b> Heatmap showing log<sub>10</sub>FC values for age-correlated mRNAs unique to each region. <b>F</b> Upset plot of age-correlated mRNAs shared across and unique to each region (each matrix column represents either DARs specific to a region (single circle with no vertical lines) or DARs shared between regions (with a vertical line connecting regions that share a given DAR). Vertical bar plots on top display the number of DARs in each combination of regions. Horizontal bar plots on the left display the total number of DARs for a given region. Sets with 5 mRNAs are shown. <b>G</b> Heatmap showing FC (old vs young) values for 73 mRNAs upregulated with age and shared between at least five regions. <b>H</b> Bar plot of <i>C4b</i> (top) and <i>Lyz2</i> (bottom) expression at indicated regions. Each dot shows the average expression of the two technical replicates for each mouse relative to the mean of the young group, equivalent to <i>n</i>=4 biological replicates per group. Data were summarized as meanstandard error of the relative mean counts. <i>p</i> values are reported from an unpaired two-tailed <i>t</i>-test with Welchs correction comparing old vs. young and middle vs. young. Note that the slope of the gray lines connecting the means is the highest in fiber tracts. Source data are provided as a Source Data file.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 2" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-chevron-right-small"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We next queried whether age-related DARs showed any spatial bias in the mouse brain. Even when visualizing all 10109 mRNAs at once, it was evident that different anatomical regions of the brain were unique in their mRNA signatures (Supp. Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM1">2FG</a>). The strip chart in Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig2">2B</a> displays DARs in each of the seven brain regions. A total of 818 DARs, significantly affected by aging, were identified in at least one region. About 544 of these DARs were uniquely assigned to only one region, while only 39 were shared across all brain regions suggesting strong spatial preference in the aging brain transcriptome (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig2">2C</a>, top). Interestingly, 505 DARs were traced to the fiber tract region (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig2">2C</a>, bottom), a region that is composed largely of myelinated axons and responsible for facilitating communication across brain regions. Some prominent tracts visible in the coronal section are the corticospinal tract and the corpus callosum. The corticospinal tract is the largest at the rostrocaudal level, encompassing the internal capsule and cerebral peduncle<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Emos, M. C., Khan Suheb, M. Z. &amp; Agarwal, S. Neuroanatomy, Internal Capsule. StatPearls (2024)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e1260">16</a></sup>. This tract connects primary motor and somatosensory cortices with subcortical structures, en route to the spinal cord. The corpus callosum connects interhemispheric cortical regions, and shows age-related decline in size, microstructural integrity, and associated cognitive function<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Reuter-Lorenz, P. A. &amp; Stanczak, L. Differential effects of aging on the functions of the corpus callosum. Dev. Neuropsychol. 18, 113137 (2000)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e1265">17</a></sup>. Most upregulated DARs were shared across brain regions (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig2">2D</a>) and were related to immune activation; 38 upregulated DARs were shared across all seven regions, 73 across at least five regions, and 183 in at least two regions, suggesting a coordinated change (Supp. Data<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM7">5</a>). Approximately 91 downregulated DARs shared across at least two brain regions were related to neuropeptide signaling and nervous system development (Supp. Data<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM7">5</a>). Among the uniquely upregulated DARs, the fiber tracts showed the highest number (<i>n</i>=177, Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig2">2E</a>, Supp. Data<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM7">5</a>), with many DARs being related to the extracellular matrix, wound healing, cell motility, and migration suggestive of ongoing injury and repair. Similarly, fiber tracts also showed the most downregulated DARs (<i>n</i>=126, Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig2">2E</a> and Supp. Data<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM7">5</a>). The upset plot in Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig2">2F</a> shows a summarized matrix layout of DARs shared across and specific to each region.</p><p>Overall, our data show that different brain regions have strong spatial preference in age-related transcriptomic changes, although there are some shared features. Of interest, the fiber tract region appears to show the strongest changes in mRNA with age.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Aging fiber tracts exhibit signs of immune activation</h3><p>In the DARs that showed shared regulation across brain regions (Supp. Data<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM6">4</a>), it was intriguing to note that they undergo the highest FC (old vs young) in the fiber tract (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig2">2G</a>). Figure<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig2">2H</a> demonstrates substantial region-dependence of two strong DARs, <i>C4b</i> and <i>Lyz2</i>, with strong age-related upregulation at fiber tracts compared to other regions. By contrast, the unique DARs showed a much weaker FC (note the log<sub>10</sub> scale in Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig2">2D, E</a>). The annotated Visium images of some shared upregulated DARs are shown in Supp. Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM1">4AR</a> highlighting the spatial bias at fiber tracts.</p><p>We next performed a region-by-region gene set enrichment analysis (GSEA) to query the functional relevance of mRNAs that are strongly enriched with age (Supp. Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM1">5</a> and Supp. Data<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM8">6</a>). All seven regions showed an enrichment in immune-related pathways in the old. By contrast, pathways related to synaptic function, particularly in the thalamus and hypothalamus and evident at the pseudobulk level (Supp. Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM1">2CE</a>) were strongly depleted. Given that fiber tracts show the strongest FC in shared age-associated mRNAs (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig2">2G</a>), we conclude that immune-modulatory mRNAs are markedly increased in the fiber tracts of aging brains.</p><p>Among the immune-modulatory mRNAs upregulated throughout the brain and particularly the fiber tract, several mRNAs related to microglia activation (<i>Lgals3</i>, <i>Aif1</i>, <i>Trem2</i>, <i>Tyrobp</i>, <i>Lyz2</i>, <i>Gpnmb</i> mRNAs), complement system activation (<i>C1q</i>, <i>C4b</i> mRNAs), and reactive astrogliosis (<i>Serpina3n</i>, <i>Gfap</i>, <i>Vim</i>, <i>Gpnmb</i> mRNAs) were evident (Figs.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig2">2H</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig3">3AC</a>). Among those previously reported, LGALS3 is strongly expressed in white matter-associated microglia (WAM)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Safaiyan, S. et al. White matter aging drives microglial diversity. Neuron 109, 11001117.e10 (2021)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e1397">18</a></sup> and forms nodular clusters in the corpus callosum of aged mice brains, while TREM2 classifies both WAM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Safaiyan, S. et al. White matter aging drives microglial diversity. Neuron 109, 11001117.e10 (2021)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e1401">18</a></sup> and the rare disease-associated microglia subtype stage 2 (DAM2)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Keren-Shaul, H. et al. A unique microglia type associated with restricting development of Alzheimers disease. Cell 169, 12761290 e17 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e1405">19</a></sup>, spatially associated with sites of AD pathology (Supp. Data<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM9">7</a>). DAM conversion occurs in two stages (DAM1 and DAM2) and stage 2 depends on TREM2/TYROBP signaling and presence of APOE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Keren-Shaul, H. et al. A unique microglia type associated with restricting development of Alzheimers disease. Cell 169, 12761290 e17 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e1413">19</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Aging fiber tracts are enriched in WAM and DAM2 signatures."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: Aging fiber tracts are enriched in WAM and DAM2 signatures.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-025-58466-2/MediaObjects/41467_2025_58466_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="Fig3" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-025-58466-2/MediaObjects/41467_2025_58466_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure 3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="861"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>A</b> Bar plot of activated microglia marker mRNAs <i>Lgals3</i>, <i>Aif1</i>, <i>Trem2</i>, <i>Tyrobp</i>, and <i>Gpnmb</i>, <b>B</b> complement pathway marker mRNAs <i>C1qa</i>, <i>C1qb</i>, and <i>C1qc</i>, and <b>C</b> activated astrocyte marker mRNAs <i>Serpina3n</i>, <i>Gfap</i>, <i>Gpnmb</i>, and <i>Vim</i> in fiber tracts of young, middle, and old mice. Each dot shows the average expression of the two technical replicates for each mouse relative to the mean of the young group, equivalent to <i>n</i>=4 biological replicates per group. Data were summarized as meanstandard error of the relative mean counts. <i>p</i> values are reported from an unpaired two-tailed <i>t</i>-test with Welchs correction comparing middle vs. young and old vs. young. <b>D</b> Heatmap showing average expression in young, middle, and old groups of four mRNA sets (set 14) from ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Safaiyan, S. et al. White matter aging drives microglial diversity. Neuron 109, 11001117.e10 (2021)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e1488">18</a></sup>. Values in the color scale are signals normalized on a per mRNA basis. <b>E</b> Visualization of average expression of WAM (<i>n</i>=29), DAM1 (<i>n</i>=77), top DAM2 (<i>n</i>=92), and SenNet (<i>n</i>=17) mRNA sets in young (Y1MB, Y2FB), middle (M2MA, M1FB), and old (O2MB, O1FB) mice using SPATA (see Methods). The WAM mRNA set is from ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Safaiyan, S. et al. White matter aging drives microglial diversity. Neuron 109, 11001117.e10 (2021)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e1508">18</a></sup>, the DAM1 and DAM2 mRNA sets are from ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Keren-Shaul, H. et al. A unique microglia type associated with restricting development of Alzheimers disease. Cell 169, 12761290 e17 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e1512">19</a></sup>, and the SenNet mRNA set is from ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Suryadevara, V. et al. SenNet recommendations for detecting senescent cells in different tissues. Nat. Rev. Mol. Cell Biol. 25, 10011023 (2024)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e1516">20</a></sup> (Supp. Data<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM9">7</a>). Values in the color scale are the expression scores of the mRNA sets. Source data are provided as a Source Data file.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 3" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-chevron-right-small"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>WAM and DAM2 share many features<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Safaiyan, S. et al. White matter aging drives microglial diversity. Neuron 109, 11001117.e10 (2021)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e1534">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Keren-Shaul, H. et al. A unique microglia type associated with restricting development of Alzheimers disease. Cell 169, 12761290 e17 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e1537">19</a></sup> including expression of phagocytic mRNAs such as CD68 (<i>Cd68</i> mRNA), C-type lectin domain containing 7A (<i>Clec7a</i> mRNA), and lysozyme (<i>Lyz2</i> mRNA), mRNAs associated with lipid metabolisms such as lipoprotein lipase (<i>Lpl</i> mRNA) and apolipoprotein E (<i>Apoe</i> mRNA), cathepsins B (<i>Ctsb</i> mRNA), S (<i>Ctss</i> mRNA), and Z (<i>Ctsz</i> mRNA), and antigen processing and presentation such as histocompatibility 2, D region locus 1 (<i>H2-D1</i> mRNA) or K region locus 1 (<i>H2-K1</i> mRNA) (Supp. Data<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM9">7</a>). Similarly, activated microglia<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Safaiyan, S. et al. White matter aging drives microglial diversity. Neuron 109, 11001117.e10 (2021)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e1576">18</a></sup> (set 2 in Supp. Data<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM9">7</a>) share some features with DAM1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Keren-Shaul, H. et al. A unique microglia type associated with restricting development of Alzheimers disease. Cell 169, 12761290 e17 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e1583">19</a></sup> (for example, ribosome-related <i>Rps</i> and <i>Rpl</i> mRNAs and <i>Aif1</i> mRNA) (Supp. Data<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM9">7</a>). To systematically assess the microglial signatures in our dataset, we calculated the normalized counts of four mRNA sets previously reported in four microglia subtypes in the aging brain by ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Safaiyan, S. et al. White matter aging drives microglial diversity. Neuron 109, 11001117.e10 (2021)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e1600">18</a></sup> (Supp. Data<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM9">7</a>). We traced set 1 mRNAs corresponding to WAM/DAM2, set 2 mRNAs corresponding to activated/DAM1 microglia, and sets 34 mRNAs corresponding to homeostatic microglia across the three age groups (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig3">3D</a>). The set 1 WAM/DAM2 mRNAs were dramatically upregulated across all brain regions in the oldest group, but more prominently in the fiber tracts. A modest increase in set 2 activated/DAM1 mRNAs was also noted across the different brain regions (except the fiber tract), suggesting a generally activated state of microglia during aging. Set 3 mRNAs, in general, did not change with age except <i>Cyba, Fcerg1</i> and <i>Tyrobp</i> mRNAs. Of interest, set 4 homeostatic mRNAs that include a set of cytokines and chemokines involved in homeostatic signaling, were strongly upregulated in the fiber tract (summarized in Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig3">3D</a>). Next, we implemented SPAtial Transcriptomic Analysis (SPATA) to visualize WAM (<i>n</i>=29), DAM1 (<i>n</i>=77), top DAM2 (<i>n</i>=92) signatures across aging. We also included the SenNet<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Suryadevara, V. et al. SenNet recommendations for detecting senescent cells in different tissues. Nat. Rev. Mol. Cell Biol. 25, 10011023 (2024)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e1629">20</a></sup> (<i>n</i>=17) focused panel, which includes a gene set commonly upregulated in senescence in the central nervous system. As shown in Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig3">3E</a>, and in congruence with Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig3">3D</a>, WAM and DAM2 mRNA sets were specifically upregulated in the fiber tract region with age. In contrast, the DAM1 signature did not show any prominent changes. Curiously, there was an upregulation of a senescence signature at the fiber tracts suggesting cells localized to this region could be senescent. Notably, 17 out of the original 37 mRNAs in the SenNet panel<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Suryadevara, V. et al. SenNet recommendations for detecting senescent cells in different tissues. Nat. Rev. Mol. Cell Biol. 25, 10011023 (2024)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e1643">20</a></sup> could be identified in our processed data.</p><p>To validate our spatial transcriptomic findings at the protein level, we performed immunofluorescence assays in young and old mouse brains. In hemisphere sections, we observed strong staining for AIF1 and LGALS3 at the corpus callosum and corticospinal tract area of old murine brains (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig4">4A</a>, note corresponding DAR images in Supp. Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM1">4G, R</a>). This positive signal was confirmed by zooming in to the corpus callosum or corticospinal tract areas where AIF1 and particularly LGALS3 showed strong age-enrichment (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig4">4BE</a>). LGALS3-AIF1-positive microglia assembled in nodular structures (clusters of 35) as previously reported for WAM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Safaiyan, S. et al. White matter aging drives microglial diversity. Neuron 109, 11001117.e10 (2021)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e1660">18</a></sup> and shown in the 3D images in Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig4">4D</a> and quantified in 4F. The activated astrocyte marker, GFAP, was also evident in the corpus callosum area of old brains (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig4">4G, H</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="WAM forms nodules at aging fiber tracts."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: WAM forms nodules at aging fiber tracts.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-025-58466-2/MediaObjects/41467_2025_58466_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="Fig4" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-025-58466-2/MediaObjects/41467_2025_58466_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure 4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="681"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p><b>A</b> Immunofluorescence microscopy showing the expression of LGALS3 (green) and AIF1 (red) -positive microglia in mouse hemisphere sections with nuclear staining with DAPI (blue). Scale bars are 500m.
