The white insets are magnified on the right and show the corpus callosum and corticospinal tracts in the young and old brain. <b>B</b> Confocal images showing expression of LGALS3 (green) and AIF1 (red) -positive microglia in the corpus callosum area. <b>C</b> Same as (<b>B</b>) except showing corticospinal tract area. For (<b>B</b>, <b>C</b>), scale bars are 50m. <b>D</b> Clipped 3D images showing LGALS3 (green) and AIF1 (red) -positive microglia in the corpus callosum of young and old mice. Scale bars are 15m. <b>E</b> Bar plot showing quantification of AIF-positive microglia in five fields from corpus callosum and corticospinal tract region per mouse (<i>n</i>=3 mice per group; average age, young=14.67 weeks, old=85.67 weeks). <b>F</b> Bar plot showing the fraction of LGALS3 and AIF1 double positive microglia nodules (18.2%) and LGALS3 and AIF1 single positive microglia (81.8%) quantified from the same fields as (<b>E</b>). Data were summarized as meanstandard error of the mean fraction value of 15 fields of three mice. <b>G</b> Confocal images showing expression of GFAP (green) in the corpus callosum. Scale bars are 50m. <b>H</b> Bar plot showing quantification of GFAP-positive cells from five fields in the corpus callosum and corticospinal tract area per mouse (<i>n</i>=3 mice per group; average age, young=14.67 weeks, old=85.67 weeks. For (<b>E</b>, <b>H</b>), data were summarized as meanstandard error of the mean cell number from 15 fields of three mice. <i>p</i> values are reported from an unpaired two-tailed <i>t</i>-test with Welchs correction comparing old vs young. Source data are provided as a Source Data file.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 4" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-chevron-right-small"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Overall, the increase of inflammatory mRNAs/proteins in the fiber tract area with age indicates a response to injury in this region, which has also been reported in disease conditions such as AD, amyotrophic lateral sclerosis (ALS), multiple sclerosis (MS), and Pelizaeus-Merzbacher disease (PMD)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Safaiyan, S. et al. White matter aging drives microglial diversity. Neuron 109, 11001117.e10 (2021)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e1749">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Keren-Shaul, H. et al. A unique microglia type associated with restricting development of Alzheimers disease. Cell 169, 12761290 e17 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e1752">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Love, S. Demyelinating diseases. J. Clin. Pathol. 59, 11511159 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e1755">21</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Evidence of loss of structural integrity in the aging fiber tracts</h3><p>One manifestation of white matter injury is demyelination. Given previous reports of demyelination events with aging<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Peters, A. The effects of normal aging on myelin and nerve fibers: a review. J. Neurocytol. 31, 581593 (2002)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e1768">22</a></sup>, and the increase with age of the highly phagocytic WAM/DAM2 population engulfing degraded myelin<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Safaiyan, S. et al. White matter aging drives microglial diversity. Neuron 109, 11001117.e10 (2021)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e1772">18</a></sup>, we looked for evidence of demyelination in our data. We found reduced staining of two myelin-related proteins, myelin basic protein and 2,3-cyclic nucleotide 3-phosphodiesterase (MBP and CNPase, respectively) with age. The decrease in signal for these proteins was evident in hemisphere sections (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig5">5A, B</a>), and the corpus callosum area (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig5">5CF</a>). In 3D images, AIF1- and LGALS3-positive microglia formed large nodules and were in direct opposition to the myelin-rich areas of the old brains (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig5">5G, H</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Evidence of loss of structural integrity in the aging fiber tracts."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5: Evidence of loss of structural integrity in the aging fiber tracts.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-025-58466-2/MediaObjects/41467_2025_58466_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="Fig5" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-025-58466-2/MediaObjects/41467_2025_58466_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure 5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="873"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p><b>A</b> Immunofluorescence microscopy of MBP (green) and AIF1 (red) -positive microglia in mouse hemisphere sections with nuclear staining with DAPI (blue). Scale bars are 500m. <b>B</b> Immunofluorescence microscopy of LGALS3 (green) and CNPase (red) proteins in the mouse brain. Scale bars are 500m. <b>C</b> Confocal images of MBP (green) and AIF1 (red) in the corpus callosum area. Scale bars are 50m. <b>D</b> Bar plot showing quantification of mean fluorescence intensity mean of MBP from five fields in the corpus callosum and corticospinal tract areas per mouse (<i>n</i>=3 mice per group; average age, young=14.67 weeks, old=85.67 weeks). <b>E</b> Confocal images of LGALS3 (green) and CNPase (red) in the corpus callosum area. Scale bars are 50m. <b>F</b> Same as (<b>D</b>) except quantification from CNPase signal. For (<b>D</b>, <b>F</b>), data were summarized as meanstandard error of the mean fluorescence intensity from 15 fields of three mice. <i>p</i> values are reported from an unpaired two-tailed <i>t</i>-test with Welchs correction comparing old vs young mice. <b>G</b> Clipped 3D images of MBP (green) and AIF1 (red) -positive microglia in the corpus callosum area. The merged images show co-localization of AIF-positive microglia and MBP-labeled myelin at different angles. Scale bars are 15m. <b>H</b> Clipped 3D images of LGALS3 (green) and CNPase (red) in the corpus callosum area. The merged images show the co-localization of LGALS3+ microglia and CNPase-labeled myelin in the old sections at different angles. Scale bars are 15m. <b>I</b> Propagator anisotropy (PA), non-Gaussianity (NG), return-to-axis probability (RTAP), return-to-origin probability (RTOP), and return-to-plane probability (RTPP) maps in a representative young sample. <b>J</b> Percent change maps of dMRI metrics between group average young and old mice. <b>K</b> Box plot showing quantification of white matter PA, NG, RTAP, RTOP, and RTPP in young and old mice. The boxes are bounded by the 25th and 75th percentile values, with the median represented as the bar in the middle. For (<b>J</b>, <b>K</b>) <i>n</i>=2 biological replicates per group. Source data are provided as a Source Data file.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 5" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-chevron-right-small"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We next used diffusion magnetic resonance imaging (dMRI) to investigate microstructural changes in the fiber tract regions with age (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig5">5IK</a>). We applied the mean apparent propagator (MAP) model<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Ozarslan, E. et al. Mean apparent propagator (MAP) MRI: a novel diffusion imaging method for mapping tissue microstructure. Neuroimage 78, 1632 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e1874">23</a></sup> to ex vivo dMRI data obtained from young and old mice (<i>n</i>=2 biological replicates per group). MAP-MRI explicitly measures the diffusion propagators (i.e., the probability density function of 3D net displacements of diffusing water molecules) in each voxel and can, therefore, capture arbitrary fiber configurations, and in particular, both normal<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Bouhrara, M., Avram, A. V., Kiely, M., Trivedi, A. &amp; Benjamini, D. Adult lifespan maturation and degeneration patterns in gray and white matter: a mean apparent propagator (MAP) MRI study. Neurobiol. Aging 124, 104116 (2023)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e1881">24</a></sup> and abnormal<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Spotorno, N. et al. Measures of cortical microstructure are linked to amyloid pathology in Alzheimers disease. Brain 146, 16021614 (2023)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e1885">25</a></sup> age-related changes. The zero-displacement parameter maps, return-to-origin probability (RTOP), return-to-axis probability (RTAP), and return-to-plane probability (RTPP), which are all inversely proportional to the spatial dimensions within the microstructure were obtained. We also mapped the non-Gaussianity (NG), which quantifies the dissimilarity between the full propagator and its Gaussian component and reflects the deviation from a simple tensor model, and the propagator anisotropy (PA), which quantifies the directional dependence of the diffusion process. These maps are shown in Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig5">5I</a> from a representative young sample. The Turone atlas (<a href="https://www.nitrc.org/docman/?group_id=1411">https://www.nitrc.org/docman/?group_id=1411</a>) was used as a common template for registration. Percent change maps of dMRI metrics between group average young and old mice are shown in Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig5">5J</a>, indicating decreases with age in the major white matter tracts, in agreement with our implication of the fiber tract injury with age and in vivo human findings<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Bouhrara, M., Avram, A. V., Kiely, M., Trivedi, A. &amp; Benjamini, D. Adult lifespan maturation and degeneration patterns in gray and white matter: a mean apparent propagator (MAP) MRI study. Neurobiol. Aging 124, 104116 (2023)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e1903">24</a></sup>. Quantitative comparisons of dMRI parameters averaged across the whole-brain white matter between the young and old groups are shown in Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig5">5K</a>. Our collective imaging data thus confirms the loss of white matter integrity in the aged brain.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Innate myeloid immune cells are increased in the old mouse brain</h3><p>ST can be used to infer cell types from complex mixtures, such as a Visium barcode spot that contains 525 cells, by comparing to a reference. We implemented the computational algorithm robust cell-type decomposition (RCTD)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Cable, D. M. et al. Robust decomposition of cell type mixtures in spatial transcriptomics. Nat. Biotechnol. 40, 517526 (2022)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e1918">26</a></sup>, which uses a supervised learning approach to deconvolute cell types while correcting for platform differences. We used as a reference a recently published single-cell dataset of the aging mouse brain from ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Ximerakis, M. et al. Single-cell transcriptomic profiling of the aging mouse brain. Nat. Neurosci. 22, 16961708 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e1922">27</a></sup> containing 25 distinct cell-type signatures. Six cell types were significantly altered with age (Supp. Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM1">6A, B</a>), primary among which were astrocyte restricted precursors (ARPs) which increased with age in the fiber tract. The presence of ARPs in the fiber tract area was interesting and suggests that they co-occur with phagocytic WAM and maybe actively targeting the myelin sheath. Mature neurons showed a trend of decrease with age in the cortical and hippocampal area (Supp. Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM1">6A</a>), although it did not reach statistical significance (Supp. Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM1">6B</a>). Oligodendrocytes showed a trend of increase with age in the fiber tract area (Supp. Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM1">6A</a>) but also did not reach statistical significance (Supp. Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM1">6B</a>).</p><p>Neurons, astrocytes, and oligodendrocytes are some of the most abundant cell types in the brain and, therefore, could be visualized easily in the brain sections (Supp. Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM1">6A</a>). Other cell types (not evident by mapping) could be quantified from their calculated RCTD normalized weights and compared across samples. We noted that innate immune myeloid cells (microglia, monocytes, dendritic cells, macrophages, and neutrophils) were selectively over-represented in the aged brain (Supp. Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM1">6B</a>). As a validation, we tested the brain for the presence of neutrophils in both male and female mice (<i>n</i>=5 biological replicates per group). Neutrophils are short-lived cells (lifespan of hours), otherwise found only in peripheral blood by flow cytometry. Importantly, we perfused the animals with PBS to remove any contaminant blood prior to brain harvest. Our data revealed increased numbers of neutrophils in the aged brain parenchyma in both males and females, with differences reaching statistical significance in females (Supp. Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM1">6CE</a>).</p><p>Since aging is accompanied by a myeloid bias in peripheral blood<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Cho, R. H., Sieburg, H. B. &amp; Muller-Sieburg, C. E. A new mechanism for the aging of hematopoietic stem cells: aging changes the clonal composition of the stem cell compartment but not individual stem cells. Blood 111, 55535561 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e1960">28</a></sup>, we questioned whether this increase in neutrophils in the brain was perhaps due to a compromise in BBB integrity. There was evidence in our data of reactive astrogliosis, marked by an increase in <i>Serpina3n</i> which encodes -1 anti-chymotrypsin (1-ACT), a serine protease inhibitor. In fact, <i>Serpina3n</i> was one of the strongest upregulated mRNAs in old brains in our dataset (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig2">2A, B</a> and Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM1">S2</a>A, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM1">S3</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM1">S4</a>C, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM1">S7</a>). 1-ACT is a critical regulator of BBB damage<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Kim, H. et al. Reactive astrocytes transduce inflammation in a blood-brain barrier model through a TNF-STAT3 signaling axis and secretion of alpha 1-antichymotrypsin. Nat. Commun. 13, 6581 (2022)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e1986">29</a></sup> and its increase typically suggests leakiness in the BBB, as has been evidenced in the aging brain and in disease context<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Marques, F., Sousa, J. C., Sousa, N. &amp; Palha, J. A. Blood-brain-barriers in aging and in Alzheimers disease. Mol. Neurodegener. 8, 38 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e1990">30</a></sup>. Thus, the presence of neutrophils in the brain is likely due to infiltration incurred from BBB damage, as has been noted in AD brains<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Zenaro, E. et al. Neutrophils promote Alzheimers disease-like pathology and cognitive decline via LFA-1 integrin. Nat. Med. 21, 880886 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e1994">31</a></sup>.</p><p>Together, our data indicate two nodes of damage in the aging brain: (1) fiber tracts with extensive demyelination and (2) BBB damage and astrogliosis with infiltration of peripheral blood cells.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Female mouse brains exhibit stronger pro-inflammatory changes</h3><p>The impact of sex on neurodegeneration is a complex and poorly understood topic, however, there are clear differences in incidence rates, clinical manifestations, and progression driven by sex hormones or genes on sex chromosomes. Our primary dataset (Figs.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig1">1</a><a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig5">5</a> and Supp. Figs.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM1">1</a><a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM1">5</a>) had representation from both sexes but was not powered to address sex-specific differences (<i>n</i>=2 per age per sex). Nevertheless, when analyzing the 73 (mostly inflammatory) mRNAs commonly upregulated with age across five brain regions (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig2">2G</a>), it was clear that females showed stronger expression than males (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig6">6A</a>, left). We next performed a priori power analysis to determine a reasonable sample size required to confidently assess sex differences in ST data. Accordingly, we added three additional biological replicates of young and old mouse brains for a total of <i>n</i>=5 per age per sex. An increase of sample size from <i>n</i>=2 to <i>n</i>=5 led to a power increase from 0.51 to 0.98, considering a difference between means of 3.5 S.D. and an FDR &lt;0.05 for identifying DARs. When analyzing the new data (<i>n</i>=3 per age per sex, Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig6">6A</a>, middle) or combined with our primary dataset (which we call cohort 2, <i>n</i>=5 per age per sex, Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig6">6A</a>, right), we noted stronger transcriptional signatures in females compared to males. These results also confirm the reproducibility of our results. Additionally, in cohort 2, we find <i>n</i>=37 out of 69 DARs were significantly higher in old females than old males (FDR &lt;0.05, and FC 1.5, labeled red in Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig6">6A</a>, right). Notably, we re-pooled the young and old libraries from our primary dataset with the three additional replicates and sequenced all libraries together for the combined analysis to minimize batch effects due to sequencing. We noted that females had more DARs compared to males (although there was a significant overlap of upregulated mRNAs) and that the fiber tracts had an overrepresentation of the upregulated DARs compared to other regions (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig6">6B, C</a>). These pro-inflammatory DARs are shown as a scatterplot in pseudobulk data from all brain regions in Supp. Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM1">7B</a> and specifically for fiber tracts in Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig6">6D</a>. A few select DARs are further graphed to show within-group variations across the 5 animals in Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig6">6E</a>. Together, our results suggest that, at least in mice, the age-related neuroinflammatory response is stronger in females at the transcriptional level.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="Female mouse brains exhibit stronger pro-inflammatory changes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 6: Female mouse brains exhibit stronger pro-inflammatory changes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-025-58466-2/MediaObjects/41467_2025_58466_Fig6_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="Fig6" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-025-58466-2/MediaObjects/41467_2025_58466_Fig6_HTML.png" alt="figure 6" loading="lazy" width="685" height="888"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p><b>A</b> Heatmap of count mean values for 73 mRNAs shared among at least five regions grouped by age and sex at pseudobulk level (left). Heatmap of count mean values of 69 of the 73 mRNAs detected in the second round of experiments with <i>n</i>=3 biological replicates per age per sex (middle). Heatmap of the count mean values of 69 of the 73 mRNAs with <i>n</i>=5 biological replicate per age per sex. <i>n</i>=37 out 69 DARs significantly higher in old females than old males (FDR &lt;0.05, and FC 1.5) are labeled red (right). <b>B</b> Bar plot showing the number of age-related DARs in female and male mice split by brain region. The age-related mRNAs were filtered by FDR &lt;0.05 and FC 1.5 or FC 1.5 in old vs. young. <b>C</b> Venn diagram showing overlap of four indicated groups of DARs in old vs. young, male and female mice in fiber tracts. <b>D</b> Scatterplot showing significant DARs in old vs. young male and female mice (FDR &lt;0.05, FC 1.5 or 1.5) in fiber tracts. <b>E</b> Bar plot of activated microglia marker mRNAs <i>Lgals3</i>, <i>Aif1</i>, <i>Trem2</i>, <i>Tyrobp</i>, and <i>Lyz2</i>; complement pathway marker mRNAs <i>C4b</i>, <i>C1qa</i>, <i>C1qb</i>, and <i>C1qc</i>; and activated astrocyte marker mRNAs <i>Serpina3n, Gfap, Gpnmb</i>, and <i>Vim</i> in fiber tracts of young and old, male and female mice. Data were summarized as meanstandard error of the relative mean counts. Each dot shows the expression in an individual animal relative to the mean of the young female group, equivalent to <i>n</i>=5 biological replicates per age per sex. <i>p</i> values are reported from a one-way ANOVA with Tukeys multiple comparisons test. Source data are provided as a Source Data file.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 6" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-chevron-right-small"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Regions other than fiber tracts that showed sex-biased expression included (among others) hippocampus, thalamus, and hypothalamus (Supp. Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM1">7CE</a> and Supp. Data<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM11">9</a>). Of mRNAs with sex-specific regulation during aging, the mRNAs encoding chloride intracellular channel 6 (<i>Clic6</i> mRNA) and transthyretin (<i>Ttr</i> mRNA), both involved in chronic psychogenic stress, are upregulated in female hippocampus with age but downregulated in males (Supp. Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM1">7C</a>). The mRNA encoding cortexin 3 (<i>Ctxn3</i> mRNA), is selectively upregulated in the thalamus of aged male mice but downregulated in females (Supp. Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM1">7D</a>). Similarly, the precerebellin mRNA (<i>Cbln1</i> mRNA), important for synapse integrity and synaptic plasticity was downregulated in females but upregulated in males in the thalamus and fiber tracts (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig6">6D</a> and Supp. Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM1">7D</a>). In contrast, antidiuretic hormone vasopressin (<i>Avp</i> mRNA) expression was upregulated in the thalamus in female mice but strongly downregulated in males (Supp. Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM1">7D</a>). In the hypothalamus, the leucine-rich repeat containing 17 mRNA (<i>Lrrc17</i> mRNA) was upregulated in male mice but downregulated in females (Supp. Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM1">7E</a>).</p><p>Overall, there are many sex-specific differences in the transcriptomic profiles of male and female aging brains, with the latter showing stronger inflammatory features.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Machine learning prediction of epigenetic drivers of gene expression in the aging mouse brain</h3><p>Gene expression is tightly regulated by the activity of transcription factors (TFs), proteins that bind to specific DNA sequences located in upstream promoter regions of genes. Therefore, we sought to build a predictive model to identify key TFs most likely to regulate gene expression changes in the aging brain (see Methods). We did this separately for each of the seven brain regions identified in our data (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig1">1C</a>). The two inputs in this process were (1) ChIP-seq data for the TFs of interest and (2) transcriptomics data for the target genes in each region. For the first input data from ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Benayoun, B. A. et al. Remodeling of epigenome and transcriptome landscapes with aging in mice reveals widespread induction of inflammatory responses. Genome Res. 29, 697709 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e2218">32</a></sup>, was a subset for TF binding, and codified into a binary format representing the presence or absence of target gene binding for each TF. For the second input, we used our ST data from this study (cohort 1 and 2 separately) categorized as up if the FC &gt;1.2, down if &lt;1.2. For cohort 1 an FDR &lt;0.05 was used whereas for cohort 2 FDR &lt;0.1 was used. Any mRNA not passing the FDR or FC filter was categorized as constant. The integrated inputs allowed for the creation of a matrix (Supp. Data<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM12">10</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Lu, R. J. et al. Multi-omic profiling of primary mouse neutrophils predicts a pattern of sex and age-related functional regulation. Nat. Aging 1, 715733 (2021)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e2225">33</a></sup> that represented the binding of the TFs to the regulatory regions of the target genes. We then trained random forest models to predict whether an mRNA is upregulated or downregulated in a given brain region using the TF binding profile as model features. Our results indicate that training on one brain region best predicts expression within that region (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig7">7A</a>, cohort 1 on top and cohort 2 on bottom), but also that trained models can generalize to other regions, arguably with lower accuracy, indicating shared features and regulatory pathways between brain regions.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-7" data-title="Machine learning prediction of epigenetic drivers of gene expression in the aging mouse brain."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig7" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 7: Machine learning prediction of epigenetic drivers of gene expression in the aging mouse brain.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2/figures/7" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-025-58466-2/MediaObjects/41467_2025_58466_Fig7_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="Fig7" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-025-58466-2/MediaObjects/41467_2025_58466_Fig7_HTML.png" alt="figure 7" loading="lazy" width="685" height="844"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-7-desc"><p><b>A</b> Heatmap showing the balanced classification accuracy of random forest models for cohort 1 (top) and cohort 2 (bottom) over three classes (up/down/constant) across seven brain regions. The final prediction was defined as up or down if at least 75% of the models agreed, as previously described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Lu, R. J. et al. Multi-omic profiling of primary mouse neutrophils predicts a pattern of sex and age-related functional regulation. Nat. Aging 1, 715733 (2021)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e2247">33</a></sup>. For cross-region validation, the entire data of the tested region was used. The color scale shows the accuracy values. <b>B</b> Scatterplot of top 50 features obtained from machine learning on cohort 1 (left) or cohort 2 (right) and Lisa ranks of predicted TFs from upregulated DARs in pseudobulk data. C/EBP and YY1, labeled in red, were chosen for testing. <b>C</b> Bar plot of <i>Cebpb</i> mRNA in fiber tracts of young, middle, and old mice. Each dot shows the average expression of the two technical replicates for each mouse relative to mean of young group, equivalent to <i>n</i>=4 biological replicates per group. Data were summarized as meanstandard error of the relative mean counts. **<i>p</i>&lt;0.01, from an unpaired two-tailed <i>t</i>-test with Welchs correction comparing middle vs. young and old vs. young (left). Schematic showing the study design of CUT&amp;RUN experiments in primary microglia isolated from young and old mouse brains (right). <b>D</b> Metaplot showing binding of C/EBP near TSSs of 73 genes upregulated during aging (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig2">2D, G</a>) and shared across five regions (top) or a background set of 91 genes that are downregulated with age also from Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig2">2D</a> (bottom). <b>E</b> Same as (<b>D</b>), except for YY1. For (<b>D</b>, <b>E</b>), ***<i>p</i>&lt;0.001 from an unpaired two-tailed <i>t</i>-test with Welchs correction comparing AUC values across the gene body in old vs young. <b>F</b> Representative genome browser tracks showing binding of C/EBP (left) and YY1 (right) at key upregulated genes. <b>G</b> Comprehensive model derived from this study showing BBB damage and demyelination at fiber tracts with consequential microglia activation (WAM/DAM2 signature), astrogliosis, and neutrophil infiltration. Illustration credit for parts of (<b>C</b>) goes to Endosymbiont GmbH, and BioRender. Sen, P. (2025) <a href="https://BioRender.com/m70w321">https://BioRender.com/m70w321</a>. Source data are provided as a Source Data file.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2/figures/7" data-track-dest="link:Figure7 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 7" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-chevron-right-small"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To identify the most informative features (most influential TFs), we ranked the TFs according to their impact on prediction performance when the corresponding TF was removed from the training set (see Methods). The top 50 most important proteins were chosen for further analysis from cohort 1 and 2 with 35 proteins shared between the two. To further increase our predictive power, we performed Lisa analysis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Qin, Q. et al. Lisa: inferring transcriptional regulators through integrative modeling of public chromatin accessibility and ChIP-seq data. Genome Biol. 21, 32 (2020)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e2326">34</a></sup>, a method that leverages a comprehensive database of ChIP-seq and DNase-seq data (CistromeDB) to identify TFs and other chromatin regulators perturbing a user-provided gene set (in our case, pseudobulk upregulated DARs). Figure<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig7">7B</a> shows a scatterplot of chromatin regulators based on their rank in Lisa and machine learning. We selected two TFs, CCAAT/enhancer binding protein  (C/EBP) and Yin Yang 1 (YY1), both known to mediate inflammatory gene expression in myeloid cells<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Lu, Y. et al. Regulation of TREM2 expression by transcription factor YY1 and its protective effect against Alzheimers disease. J. Biol. Chem. 299, 104688 (2023)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e2333">35</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Troutman, T. D., Kofman, E. &amp; Glass, C. K. Exploiting dynamic enhancer landscapes to decode macrophage and microglia phenotypes in health and disease. Mol. Cell 81, 38883903 (2021)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e2336">36</a></sup>, for further validation. Additionally, <i>Cebpb</i> was among the unique mRNAs upregulated with age in the fiber tract (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig7">7C</a>, left and Supp. Data<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM7">5</a>).</p><p>We isolated primary microglia from young and old brains (<i>n</i>=3 biological replicates per age per sex) using a bead-based method and performed Cleavage Under Targets and Release Using Nuclease (CUT&amp;RUN)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Skene, P. J. &amp; Henikoff, S. An efficient targeted nuclease strategy for high-resolution mapping of DNA binding sites. Elife 6, e21856 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e2356">37</a></sup> with C/EBP or YY1 antibodies (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig7">7C</a>, right). Control reactions were incubated with IgG antibodies. Binding patterns of both TFs revealed strong enrichments near TSSs of the 73 upregulated genes shared across five regions from Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig2">2D, G</a> (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig7">7D, E</a>, top panels). C/EBP showed a statistically significant increase in binding with age, while YY1 showed a slight (but significant) decrease, although the effect sizes were small. In contrast, we observed much weaker binding at the 91 downregulated genes from Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig2">2D</a> (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig7">7D, E</a>, bottom panels), which was not statistically different between the age groups. Some representative browser shots of C/EBP and YY1 binding are shown in Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig7">7F</a> (from the gene set in Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig7">7D, E top</a>). While most of the genes were co-bound with C/EBP and YY1, some, such as <i>Lgals3</i>, were exclusively bound by C/EBP (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig7">7F</a>, top). More importantly, we noted that both TFs were bound even in the young state, suggesting that inflammatory genes are primed for expression by the pre-binding of critical TFs. Further work is needed to elucidate the role of coactivator recruitment by these TFs during brain aging.</p></div></div></section><section data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec11-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec11">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec11-content"><p>In this study, we performed systematic, spatiotemporal mapping of the aging mouse brain spanning three age groups (young, middle, and old), both sexes (male and female), and two technical replicates per animal (A and B) for a total of 24 high-quality, high-depth, reproducible datasets (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig1">1</a> and Supp. Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM1">1</a>). This initial cohort data covered a total of 67,998 spatial spots, with an average tissue coverage of 2833, ~56K mean reads, and ~5219 median genes per spot. The average coverage of 2833 out of 5000 spots within a Visium capture area represents ~56% and aligns with Image J estimates of tissue coverage for a single coronal hemisphere section. These parameters were comparable in cohort 2 where we increased the sample size for estimation of sex differences (total <i>n</i>=5 per age per sex, Supp. Data<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM4">2</a>) for a total of 36 Visium libraries combining both cohorts. This benchmarking process lent confidence to our sample preparation step.</p><p>We found that it was possible to develop a detailed whole-brain spatial annotation based purely on gene expression signatures (Figs.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig1">1</a>BF, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig2">2C</a>, top). Using a pseudobulk approach first, we identified a strong immune signature in the aging brain (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig2">2</a> and Supp. Figs.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM1">2</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM1">5</a>) as has been reported in numerous previous studies (reviewed in ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Lucin, K. M. &amp; Wyss-Coray, T. Immune activation in brain aging and neurodegeneration: too much or too little? Neuron 64, 110122 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e2428">9</a></sup>). To unambiguously discern DARs that emerge due to age, we incorporated a middle-age group as a design feature in our study in this initial cohort. Our spatial data was thus able to derive all possible differential gene expression trajectories across the lifespan (Supp. Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM1">3</a>). We found 37 DARs were upregulated from middle-age onwards (Supp. Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM1">3</a>, clusters 12) while 112 DARs in old age (Supp. Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM1">3</a>, cluster 4). Downregulated DARs were fewer with 23 DARs that declined from middle-age (Supp. Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM1">3</a>, clusters 910). Additionally, we found smaller DAR sets that were exclusively up- or down-regulated in middle-age (Supp. Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM1">3</a>, clusters 57 (up), <i>n</i>=22 and clusters 3 and 8 (down), <i>n</i>=7). Importantly, we found that the age-related immune signature (primarily cluster 4) was most prominent in the oldest group (average age 126 weeks) while being only modestly upregulated in the middle-age group (average age 57.5 weeks) (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig2">2</a> and Supp. Figs.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM1">2</a><a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM1">7</a>), suggesting that inflammatory changes in the brain occur late in life. Furthermore, we found that a smaller group of mRNAs related to synaptic function was downregulated with age (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig2">2</a> and Supp. Figs.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM1">2</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM1">3</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM1">5</a>), suggesting a loss of brain connectivity. We speculate the reason we find more upregulated than downregulated DARs is because neuroinflammation is an active response, driven by TFs (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig7">7</a>). Additionally, we find that at least two tested TFs, C/EBP and YY1, are already bound to TSS regions of upregulated DARs in the young, suggesting these genes are primed to launch a robust response. In turn, this suggests that glia-mediated clearance mechanisms are programmatically activated in the aging brain. In contrast, the weaker downregulation of synaptic genes is indicative of secondary degenerative processes in the neurons.</p><p>Our dataset thus presents a systematic, reproducible view of age-related neuroinflammation and reflects a snapshot of processes likely to be physiologically relevant. Most single-cell investigations of the aging or diseased brain involve making single-cell suspensions from tissue homogenates, with the loss of relevant cellular connections through ligand-receptor pathways. In fact, a previous seminal study<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Bohlen, C. J. et al. Diverse requirements for microglial survival, specification, and function revealed by defined-medium cultures. Neuron 94, 759773 e8 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e2482">38</a></sup> reported that mature microglia rapidly lose their identity after isolation but regain it after engraftment back into the central nervous system environment, suggesting that continuous niche input is required to maintain its structure and function. Other studies have used microglia and astrocytic cultures in a serum-containing medium, which dramatically alter expression profiles and phagocytic activity, perhaps mitigating differences due to age<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Bohlen, C. J. et al. Diverse requirements for microglial survival, specification, and function revealed by defined-medium cultures. Neuron 94, 759773 e8 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e2486">38</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Prah, J. et al. A novel serum free primary astrocyte culture method that mimic quiescent astrocyte phenotype. J. Neurosci. Methods 320, 5063 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e2489">39</a></sup>. Our data, in contrast, overcomes these challenges with direct expression profiling in situ and, therefore, shows many statistically significant changes with robust fold changes. Notably, we find some mRNAs not reported in prior single-cell studies of the aging brain<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Ximerakis, M. et al. Single-cell transcriptomic profiling of the aging mouse brain. Nat. Neurosci. 22, 16961708 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e2493">27</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Hajdarovic, K. H. et al. Single-cell analysis of the aging female mouse hypothalamus. Nat. Aging 2, 662678 (2022)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e2496">40</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Murdock, M. H. &amp; Tsai, L. H. Insights into Alzheimers disease from single-cell genomic approaches. Nat. Neurosci. 26, 181195 (2023)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e2499">41</a></sup>. For example, <i>Gpnmb</i>, an mRNA upregulated with age in our dataset, is a transmembrane senescence marker recently reported in the context of atherosclerosis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Suda, M. et al. Senolytic vaccination improves normal and pathological age-related phenotypes and increases lifespan in progeroid mice. Nat. Aging 1, 11171126 (2021)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e2506">42</a></sup> and found to be elevated in microglia in AD mouse models and human patients, along with TREM2 and APOE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Huttenrauch, M. et al. Glycoprotein NMB: a novel Alzheimers disease associated marker expressed in a subset of activated microglia. Acta Neuropathol. Commun. 6, 108 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e2511">43</a></sup>. CCAAT/enhancer binding protein delta (<i>Cebpd</i> mRNA), also upregulated in our dataset, is an innate immune player implicated in AD<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Ko, C. Y. et al. CCAAT/enhancer binding protein delta (CEBPD) elevating PTX3 expression inhibits macrophage-mediated phagocytosis of dying neuron cells. Neurobiol. Aging 33, 422 e11422 e25 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e2518">44</a></sup>. Other examples are protein tyrosine phosphatase non-receptor type 18 (<i>Ptpn18</i> mRNA), coiled-coil domain containing 3 (<i>Ccdc3</i> mRNA), and Strawberry notch homolog 2 (<i>Sbno2</i> mRNA).</p><p>Interestingly, differential mRNA abundance analysis on each individual brain region identified from the spatial profiles highlighted the fiber tract as a particularly sensitive area, showing not only the largest number of DARs but also the largest FC in expression (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig2">2</a> and Supp. Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM1">2</a>). Aging fiber tracts have been implicated as a vulnerable region in an independent spatial study while this manuscript was in preparation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Hahn, O. et al. Atlas of the aging mouse brain reveals white matter as vulnerable foci. Cell 186, 41174133 e22 (2023)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e2540">45</a></sup> and were previously identified as a degenerative center in MRI measures of myelin content and axonal density<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Bouhrara, M. et al. Age-related estimates of aggregate g-ratio of white matter structures assessed using quantitative magnetic resonance neuroimaging. Hum. Brain Mapp. 42, 23622373 (2021)." href="#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e2544">46</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Walker, K. A. et al. MRI and fluid biomarkers reveal determinants of myelin and axonal loss with aging. Ann. Clin. Transl. Neurol. 10, 397407 (2023)." href="#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e2544_1">47</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Lawrence, K. E. et al. Age and sex effects on advanced white matter microstructure measures in 15,628 older adults: a UK biobank study. Brain Imaging Behav. 15, 28132823 (2021)." href="#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e2544_2">48</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Sullivan, E. V. &amp; Pfefferbaum, A. Diffusion tensor imaging and aging. Neurosci. Biobehav. Rev. 30, 749761 (2006)." href="#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e2544_3">49</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Schilling, K. G. et al. Aging and white matter microstructure and macrostructure: a longitudinal multi-site diffusion MRI study of 1218 participants. Brain Struct. Funct. 227, 21112125 (2022)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e2547">50</a></sup>. Our dMRI results showed significant age-related decreases in white matter for the PA and RTAP indices (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig5">5IK</a>), suggesting reduced fiber orientational coherence, and an increase in microscopic water displacement, respectively, with age. These findings support the microstructural scenario of axonal degradation with age, leading to increased extracellular volume, in which water diffuses almost freely with little anisotropy but with a diffusivity similar to that of cerebrospinal fluid (CSF). Additionally, a recent publication using multiplexed error-robust fluorescent in situ hybridization (MERFISH), combined with single-nucleus RNA sequencing (snRNA-seq), identified the white matter as a hotspot for age-related glial and immune cell activation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Allen, W. E., Blosser, T. R., Sullivan, Z. A., Dulac, C. &amp; Zhuang, X. Molecular and spatial signatures of mouse brain aging at single-cell resolution. Cell 186, 194208 e18 (2023)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e2555">51</a></sup>. Thus, our data, together with results from independent studies across diverse platforms, pinpoints fiber tracts as focal points of brain aging.</p><p>Why might the white matter be a hotspot for inflammation? We believe that white matter is an early target during aging due to its unique macromolecular composition, vascularity, cellular composition, and structure. The white matter is rich in lipids, forming lipid peroxide products upon oxidation that are highly damaging<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Chia, L. S., Thompson, J. E. &amp; Moscarello, M. A. Changes in lipid phase behaviour in human myelin during maturation and aging. Involvement of lipid peroxidation. FEBS Lett. 157, 155158 (1983)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e2563">52</a></sup>. The white matter is less vascularized than gray matter<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Thomas, B. P., Liu, P., Park, D. C., van Osch, M. J. &amp; Lu, H. Cerebrovascular reactivity in the brain white matter: magnitude, temporal characteristics, and age effects. J. Cereb. Blood Flow. Metab. 34, 242247 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e2567">53</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Juttukonda, M. R. et al. Characterizing cerebral hemodynamics across the adult lifespan with arterial spin labeling MRI data from the Human Connectome Project-Aging. Neuroimage 230, 117807 (2021)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e2570">54</a></sup> making it also susceptible to hypoxic damage. It is also possible that oligodendrocytes or oligodendrocyte progenitor cells (OPCs) in the fiber tract area may become senescent, releasing senescence-associated secretory phenotype (SASP) factors, and attracting microglia. Although not directly tested, we find a modest senescence signature in the fiber tract area (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig3">3E</a>). Additionally, senescent OPCs have been found around A plaques in AD mouse models and human brains. Finally, complement proteins, APOE and LGALS3, can serve as opsonins<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Yu, F. et al. Phagocytic microglia and macrophages in brain injury and repair. CNS Neurosci. Ther. 28, 12791293 (2022)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e2577">55</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Diaz-Alvarez, L. &amp; Ortega, E. The many roles of galectin-3, a multifaceted molecule, in innate immune responses against pathogens. Mediators Inflamm. 2017, 9247574 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e2580">56</a></sup>, coating the white matter tracts for microglia targeting. Our snapshot view may be capturing this ongoing event. Relatedly, we find many more upregulated DARs in this area than downregulated DARs, with fewer GO hits for downregulated mRNAs (Supp. Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM1">5</a>). It is possible that when it comes to upregulation, there is a signal amplification and spreading process at fiber tracts. For example, it is possible that senescent oligodendrocytes induce bystander senescence which releases more SASP. Or that there is a high concentration of myelin debris near fiber tracts that can cause chronic microglial activation, which in turn activates other nearby microglia. Chronic exposures are also associated with microglial priming<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Perry, V. H. &amp; Holmes, C. Microglial priming in neurodegenerative disease. Nat. Rev. Neurol. 10, 217224 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e2588">57</a></sup>, an exaggerated response to a secondary stimulus. In contrast, downregulated DARs, mostly related to synaptic function, are secondary degenerative effects that are not well-controlled responses and do not have an amplification component.</p><p>The mRNA signature at fiber tracts (and to a lesser extent in the hippocampus, thalamus, and hypothalamus) had three major indications: microglia activation, astrogliosis, and innate immune myeloid cell infiltration (summarized in Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig7">7G</a>). Accumulation and activation of innate immune cells in heavily myelinated fiber tracts in the old brain underscore the involvement of immune cells in demyelination/remyelination or myelin debris removal during natural aging. Previous literature has reported that aging and neurodegeneration activate distinct microglia subtypes: WAM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Safaiyan, S. et al. White matter aging drives microglial diversity. Neuron 109, 11001117.e10 (2021)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e2598">18</a></sup> and DAM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Keren-Shaul, H. et al. A unique microglia type associated with restricting development of Alzheimers disease. Cell 169, 12761290 e17 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e2602">19</a></sup>. In mouse models of AD, the WAM signature appears early, followed by gradual upregulation of DAM1 and DAM2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Safaiyan, S. et al. White matter aging drives microglial diversity. Neuron 109, 11001117.e10 (2021)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e2606">18</a></sup>. The high phagocytic activity of DAM2 triggered in the disease context serves to clear degraded myelin and is said to be protective. We investigated WAM and DAM expression signatures and could identify both as being activated in our dataset (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig4">4</a>). In particular, <i>Trem2, Tyrobp</i>, <i>Lgals3</i> mRNA, and early complement pathway mRNAs <i>C1q</i> and <i>C4b</i> were remarkably upregulated in microglia in old white matter, implicating their involvement in aging-related fiber tract pathophysiology.</p><p>TREM2 promotes myelin debris removal by microglia in naturally aging brains, highlighting its neuroprotective role<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Safaiyan, S. et al. White matter aging drives microglial diversity. Neuron 109, 11001117.e10 (2021)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e2629">18</a></sup>. However, TREM2 was also shown to convert microglia phenotype from homeostatic to neurodegenerative, and ablation of TREM2 in AD mice resulted in suppression of inflammatory reaction<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Krasemann, S. et al. The TREM2-APOE pathway drives the transcriptional phenotype of dysfunctional microglia in neurodegenerative diseases. Immunity 47, 56658V (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e2633">58</a></sup>. Furthermore, in humans, <i>TREM2</i> variants posea risk for ADRD<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Guerreiro, R. et al. TREM2 variants in Alzheimers disease. N. Engl. J. Med. 368, 117127 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e2640">59</a></sup>. Whether TREM2/TYROBP signaling in the microglia of old brains, particularly in fiber tracts, serves a neuroprotective or neurodegenerative role, or both, remains to be determined<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Murdock, M. H. &amp; Tsai, L. H. Insights into Alzheimers disease from single-cell genomic approaches. Nat. Neurosci. 26, 181195 (2023)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e2644">41</a></sup>.</p><p>Similarly, elevated LGALS3, a WAM marker, was largely shown to be detrimental in the brain, with an implied protective function depending on the context of the surrounding microenvironment<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Garcia-Revilla, J. et al. Galectin-3, a rising star in modulating microglia activation under conditions of neurodegeneration. Cell Death Dis. 13, 628 (2022)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e2651">60</a></sup>. Activated but not homeostatic microglia express LGALS3<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Krasemann, S. et al. The TREM2-APOE pathway drives the transcriptional phenotype of dysfunctional microglia in neurodegenerative diseases. Immunity 47, 56658V (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e2655">58</a></sup>. LGALS3 was increased in neurodegenerative microglia in AD patients, and AD and MS mouse models, and ablation of LGALS3 suppressed inflammatory response and alleviated AD or MS phenotypes in mice<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Krasemann, S. et al. The TREM2-APOE pathway drives the transcriptional phenotype of dysfunctional microglia in neurodegenerative diseases. Immunity 47, 56658V (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e2659">58</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Boza-Serrano, A. et al. Galectin-3, a novel endogenous TREM2 ligand, detrimentally regulates inflammatory response in Alzheimers disease. Acta Neuropathol. 138, 251273 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e2662">61</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Jiang, H. R. et al. Galectin-3 deficiency reduces the severity of experimental autoimmune encephalomyelitis. J. Immunol. 182, 11671173 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e2665">62</a></sup>, emphasizing neurodegenerative effects of LGALS3 in the context of AD or MS. Furthermore, the proinflammatory action of LGALS3 was shown to be mediated by TREM2/TYROBP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Boza-Serrano, A. et al. Galectin-3, a novel endogenous TREM2 ligand, detrimentally regulates inflammatory response in Alzheimers disease. Acta Neuropathol. 138, 251273 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e2669">61</a></sup> or TLR4<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Burguillos, M. A. et al. Microglia-secreted galectin-3 acts as a Toll-like receptor 4 ligand and contributes to microglial activation. Cell Rep. 10, 16261638 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e2673">63</a></sup> in the brain, where LGALS3 functions as an endogenous paracrine ligand of these receptors. However, LGALS3 was required for M2 polarization in peripheral lung or peritoneal macrophages<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="MacKinnon, A. C. et al. Regulation of alternative macrophage activation by galectin-3. J. Immunol. 180, 26502658 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e2678">64</a></sup>, suggesting its protective anti-inflammatory potential in macrophages. We found that <i>Lgals3</i> expression is strikingly upregulated in microglia, and about 20% of LGALS3-positive microglia form nodules that physically interact with myelin in fiber tracts in the old brain (Figs.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig3">3</a><a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#Fig5">5</a>). We infer that LGALS3 and TREM2 are involved in the deterioration of white matter myelin sheath during natural brain aging separately or in combination.</p><p>C1q was previously reported to increase in the aging brain<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Stephan, A. H. et al. A dramatic increase of C1q protein in the CNS during normal aging. J. Neurosci. 33, 1346013474 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e2694">65</a></sup>, and we found evidence of early complement activation, with the expression of <i>C1q</i> and <i>C4b</i> mRNAs. The C1q-induced classical complement pathway plays a critical role in synaptic pruning<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Wang, C. et al. Microglia mediate forgetting via complement-dependent synaptic elimination. Science 367, 688694 (2020)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e2704">66</a></sup>, and C1q-ablated mice exhibit improved synaptic plasticity and alleviation of cognitive and memory decline during aging<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Stephan, A. H. et al. A dramatic increase of C1q protein in the CNS during normal aging. J. Neurosci. 33, 1346013474 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e2708">65</a></sup>. In MS, early complement pathway proteins were shown to be increased around progressive white matter lesions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Ingram, G. et al. Complement activation in multiple sclerosis plaques: an immunohistochemical analysis. Acta Neuropathol. Commun. 2, 53 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e2713">67</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Loveless, S. et al. Tissue microarray methodology identifies complement pathway activation and dysregulation in progressive multiple sclerosis. Brain Pathol. 28, 507520 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e2716">68</a></sup>, suggesting an involvement of the complement pathway in MS pathophysiology, i.e., demyelination and myelin debris removal<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Morgan, B. P., Gommerman, J. L. &amp; Ramaglia, V. An outside-in and inside-out consideration of complement in the multiple sclerosis brain: lessons from development and neurodegenerative diseases. Front. Cell Neurosci. 14, 600656 (2020)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e2720">69</a></sup>. Consistently, the complement proteins, APOE and LGALS3 are known to opsonize the myelin sheath in white matter to promote recognition and clustering of microglia in this region for targeted phagocytosis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Diaz-Alvarez, L. &amp; Ortega, E. The many roles of galectin-3, a multifaceted molecule, in innate immune responses against pathogens. Mediators Inflamm. 2017, 9247574 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e2724">56</a></sup>. Collectively, activated microglia and the classical complement pathway likely contribute to myelin degeneration in white matter during natural brain aging.</p><p>Another important feature evident in our data was reactive astrogliosis, which suggests a compromise of the extracellular matrix and BBB integrity, although this was not directly tested in this study<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Kim, H. et al. Reactive astrocytes transduce inflammation in a blood-brain barrier model through a TNF-STAT3 signaling axis and secretion of alpha 1-antichymotrypsin. Nat. Commun. 13, 6581 (2022)." href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d106876184e2731">29</a></sup>. Loss of BBB integrity with age facilitates infiltration of peripheral blood cells into the brain parenchyma, relegating the immune-privileged status of the brain. Using computation (RCTD) and experimentation (flow cytometry), we detected an increased myeloid cell signature and infiltration of neutrophils in the aged brain (Supp. Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-025-58466-2#MOESM1">6</a>).
